  12. I love the Sonicare. For personal oral hygiene and tooth care there is no equal. My father is a retired Dentist and gave me one of the first models when they came out in the mid to late nineties. It finally gave out on me this year, so it lasted for 13+ years (and probably would have gone much longer if I hadn't left it on the charger unplugged so many times). I am a Dental Lab Technician and have a pretty thorough understanding of the \\"oral environment\\". Regular brushing with a standard tooth brush is good, but the raking or dragging motion can damage gum tissue and periodontal ligaments that actually hold the tooth in your bone, creating pockets where bacteria can accumulate leading to potential decay at the root. Especially if you are using a hard or stiff bristle brush. When you use a Sonicare, it may feel weird at first (it really kinda' tickles), but you aren't dragging the brush across your teeth. Instead you are treating your teeth, and more importantly your gum's, to a gentle \\"massage\\" that stimulates tissues, encouraging them to be healthy and strong. Also, when using a standard brush there is a lot of food particles and plaque that you will simply not be able to get at. The bristles just graze over them. After using a Sonicare, I feel like I've just gotten a professional cleaning without all the pain. It gets into the hard to clean crevices and pockets and vibrates all the unwanted stuff out. I am also a smoker and I have found that with regular use it reduces tarter buildup that in the past had to be removed by a Hygienist with a scaler. Mind you - in NO way does ANY kind of brush replace a professional cleaning. I've tried a couple of other powered brushes on the market, and they're ok. They're more affordable, but basically do the same thing as a standard tooth brush. I admit they are a little bit better, but they still rely on a lot of movement. Nothing gets the job done like a Sonicare. Regarding the model I recently purchased: The design has improved since I received my first Sonicare in the late nineties. My old one had a tendency to \\"rattle\\" at times, which was often alarming and uncomfortable. The more compact head on the new model eliminates this problem. I saw that there were other models available that had more buttons, more lights and an optional UV cleaning unit. None of that stuff is necessary. I say keep it simple. You're more likely to use it, and used properly* a Sonicare will definitely improve your dental health and reduce costly dental bills. I make teeth for a living, so really - every time someone buys a Sonicare, I'm looking at a career change. And so is your Dentist. No joke. *Using a Sonicare \\"properly\\" does not mean you use it exactly as directed in the instructions. I spend more time on each quadrant of my teeth than the timer allows for. The important thing to me is to not abuse your teeth. Do what feels good.

This model differs from every other Sonicare model in that the magnetically driven mechanism is partially incorporated into the screw-on brush head itself (the more expensive, slimmer models use a compact head that simply clicks into a metal rod). There are more crevices in this type of head in which fluids and old toothpaste can gather. If you don't make a regular habit of cleaning the head, it can get quite disgusting. If you are an open mouth brusher, then make sure you use a thick toothpaste to minimize splatter. I originally had problems with mold forming on the seal of the brush head. I replaced the brush and began shaking the water out by turning the brush on and shaking it upside down until you know longer see little droplets of water flying out from the seal area. I began doing this every time I used the brush, and I haven't had a mold problem since. It is also very effective at drying out the bristles of the brush, thus eliminating the need to flick it with your finger and bang it on the edge of the sink. You should still remove the brush head and clean it weekly as the manufacturer says to do.

I've been using the magnetic drive Sonicares for quite a long time now, and I've had my current one, still going strong, for at least 10 years (it's approximately like the current Essence 5300). My wife bought one of the flex models, with a metal drive pin that goes into the snap-on head. These things fail all the time, usually sometime during the second year. I think it's because the metal pin moves to transduce the vibration to the head. In the purely magnetic models, only the replaceable head vibrates. It is very true that these get rather disgusting with accumulated slime over time, and the secret is to take the head off after every use. If toothpaste gets dried on there, just rinse it off in the sink. Both types can do a wonderful job on your teeth, and the pin-drive brushes do have a couple of extra modes that some might find useful. But the magnetic-drive E-style bases, with no moving parts, are the f****ng energizer bunny in terms of durability.

  412. The Sonicare line of electric toothbrushes are revolutionary. Made by Philips (a well-known manufacturer of electronic devices), these are unlike any other brush on the market because they use \\"sonic\\" or ultrasonic energy to clean teeth, gums, tongue, roof of the mouth, etc.<br /><br />This technology has been around for a long time, ultrasonic cleaners have been used to clean delicate instruments, tools, fragile or easily damaged items that couldn't be cleaned with conventional methods (brushing, harsh solvents or chemical cleaners, abrasives, etc.). Jewelers have been using it to clean delicate jewelery for decades.<br /><br />The brush ulilzes it by using high frequency magnetic oscillations generated by the handle and converts them to mechanical energy through magnets attached to steel tension arms inside the screw-on brush heads. Take off the brush head and turn the handle on and you can hear the ultrasonic energy as a faint whining noise. This energy generates around 3000 oscilations per minute...so fast you can barely detect them with the naked eye.<br /><br />The vibrations clean teeth by scrubbing action and the ultrasonic energy, which is superior to conventional and some other electric brushes because it gets into the tiny crevices in between teeth, below the gumline, and cleans the tongue. This is good because it's low-impact (the brush does the work so you don't have to brush \\"hard\\" which is bad for your teeth and gums as any Dentist will tell you) and gentle on your gums. The small brush head is able to get into the very back of the mouth to get the hard-to-reach teeth.<br /><br />The vibrations also remove stains by breaking up and polishing away the tiny molecules that turned teeth dark and removes plague and tarter the same way, and it works great!<br /><br />My Dentist says my teeth and gums are very healthy. After a couple months of using the Sonicare, my teeth got noticably whiter, my breath freshened up, and my gums (which were already healthy) got healthier!<br /><br />The unit came with the handle, a charger, and a brush head assembly which is the magnetic tension system, a flexible brush stalk and small brush head which screws onto the handle. The unit has a built-in timer system that will turn the brush off to let you know to switch from the top teeth to the bottom teeth (2 minutes each. It has a rechargable NiCad battery, and when the battery is running low, it will beep during use to let you know to charge it. I got about 4 days of use before I had to charge it. I've gone as long as 5 before, but then it will shut itself off. Charging takes 6-8 hours (overnight) but you can use it if you need to after 2.<br /><br />The brush heads aren't cheap ($30 for 2), but will last a while. They say to replace them after 3, but I've used them for 9 months before without any problems. Once you get the muscle memory you will not even think of it anymore. This prevents mold from growing in the base of the brush unit (in my experience). This is a mechanical and electrical item and therefore will need some maintenance. Mainly a recharge about once a month for me. The button will illuminate and beep to let you know it is time to recharge (which takes just a few hours on the recharge base). The brush will wear eventually, so about once a year I will replace the head. You can buy them in packs of three and then you are probably set for the useful life of the unit. The travel case works well and comes in handy. I have used it many times for travel. Most importantly, my teeth are very clean. In a pinch if the battery runs down, the unit can still be used as a regular toothbrush.

For trips up to 10 days, I do not need the charger, but I took it on a longer overseas trip and fried it; I am so used to modern electronic equipment handling 110-240 Volts and 50-60 Hz that I failed to check.<br /><br />(2) The battery is not replaceable. This means (a) added expense for the consumer, (b) wasted time for the consumer if he dutifully goes through the awkward process to remove the battery to recycle it safely, and (c) a poorer environmental footprint for the product, especially when consumers just toss it in the trash and the battery ends up in a landfill, which it should not.<br /><br />I am unaware of a justification for this decision; it is not like 2009-2010 MacBook Pro batteries that were an odd shape squeezed in around other components for the largest possible battery size; this battery CAN be replaced with a standard rechargeable battery (with difficulty, and a soldering iron!) if you follow instructions posted on YouTube, etc.<br /><br />DISHONEST REBATE<br /><br />What prompted me to write this review was the fact that Amazon told me I would \\"Receive $5 from Philips\\" as a rebate. However, when I actually filled in the rebate form, I realized that this was untrue; I would only receive $5 off if I made a further purchase of a Philips product from a very short list. If I did not need any of those products, I would not receive the $5 that Amazon promised me. (There were also $10 and $15 rebates on more expensive/higher-profit-margin products.)<br /><br />I am disappointed that Amazon would use this type of dishonest marketing, which can still be seen at the time I write this. (Go to [[ASIN:B003QYFVRY Philips Sonicare HX5351/30 Essence 5300 Rechargeable Electric Toothbrush]] and click the link \\"Rebate Forms for Recent Purchases.\\" This shows the Amazon offer of $5, but when you click the link \\"Philips Rebate Form\\" you see what you actually get from Philips.)<br /><br />I have complained to Amazon, and I hope they will take some action to restore my previously high opinion of them.<br /><br />A $5 rebate is not a big deal; it is the deceiving of customers that angers me.

  781. Compared to some of the earlier models of Sonicare I have used, this one is particularly sturdy. The brush heads you put on are larger and screw in place with a large, easy to hold plastic bit, rather than snapping onto a small piece of metal that has a tendency of coming loose. Also, unlike the earlier types I referred to, there is none of the tendency of that piece of metal to get covered in layers of toothpaste that prevent the brush head from fitting properly.<br /><br />The toothbrush is very effective. It vibrates enough to make brushing seriously more efficient than doing it by hand, but not so much as to cause any pain or damage.<br /><br />From what I have read, the Sonicare system actually does do a decent job of periodontal plaque cleaning, although it is of course not a perfect substitute for flossing. If despite your dentist's admonitions, you do not floss, you definitely want one of these. If you do floss, you may still want it, because it is convenient and using both Sonicare and floss is obviously going to clean things out even better than either by itself.<br /><br />The toothbrush comes with a sturdy travel case. The travel case does not fit the charger, but you don't usually need the charger for short trips, because the battery capacity is pretty good and you can usually use the thing for a week without charging with no problems. Worst come to worst, you can of course always use it as an old fashioned manual toothbrush.<br /><br />The charger is a bit sturdier, too, than earlier models of Sonicare charger I have used, and here too, Philips has addressed the problem of toothpaste and grime buildup. On the earlier model, over time the charger would grow a thick enough layer of toothpaste/grime/whatever buildup that the toothbrush would no longer fit on it properly, and you had to carefully clean it. This charger does not appear to share that problem.<br /><br />In general, I am quite impressed with Philips personal care products, including their Norelco line of electrical razors and trimmers. And this toothbrush is no exception. It's an excellent tool for keeping your mouth from smelling bad, your teeth from looking bad, and your dentist from having to tell you bad news.
